<div id="review_655337419" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/655337419" />
    <a title="Florencia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia"><img alt="Florencia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601866797p2/21575078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/655337419?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Florencia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Florencia" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia">Florencia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9789875505735" href="/book/show/50291.La_muerte_de_Iv_n_Ilich">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Any human being</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=russian">russian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer655337419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12796052007493279548">I read this novella a couple of weeks ago and I did not write a review right away; I had to put my thoughts in order (they rarely are but, oh well). That only happens after reading an amazing book, brilliantly written, that deals with the human condition like Dostoyevsky's keen eye can deal. This book is about life itself, life in its most virtuous and degrading glory. This masterpiece has no more than 120 pages, but it manages to show many perspectives on different issues concerning the human n</span>
  <span id="freeText12796052007493279548" style="display:none">I read this novella a couple of weeks ago and I did not write a review right away; I had to put my thoughts in order (they rarely are but, oh well). That only happens after reading an amazing book, brilliantly written, that deals with the human condition like Dostoyevsky's keen eye can deal. This book is about life itself, life in its most virtuous and degrading glory. This masterpiece has no more than 120 pages, but it manages to show many perspectives on different issues concerning the human nature; it is insane. <br /><br />Life without meaning. A hollow, immoral life that, from a certain perspective, seems even worse than death. A first sign of that meaning appears, ironically, when life is about to end. So human. <br /><br />This book starts with Ivan's death. Every human reaction described by Tolstoy is too damn real. Every passage has a different idea, a different way of describing real human behavior. A person just died and his allegedly best friend is thinking about getting out of his room so he can play cards with another fella. And don't get me started on the widow... A life vanished and this shallow people can't stop thinking about themselves.<br /><br />Ivan Ilyich achieved an important social status after years of work, pushing his family aside. A man that thought he had lived well, was now suffering a painful death. However, is this death more painful than the way he actually lived? Did he live, at all? The eternal questions remain unanswered: what is exactly “to live”? What's the meaning of all that? Why are we here? Why can't you tickle yourself? What's a number? Can we really be objective? Will airplane food get any better? Will celebrities ever stop naming their kids after inanimate objects?<br />OK, moving on.<br /><br />There are many things I would like to say about this book but I just can't. I don't want to spoil this anymore. And I know you can read a lot about it in any other place, but not in here.<br /><br />I never ask anyone to read anything. However, I urge you, and you, and the other one next to you... to read this book.<br /><br /><br />Mar 05, 14<br />* Also on <a href="https://nonbookreviews.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">my blog</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12796052007493279548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_270220964" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/270220964" />
    <a title="Rowena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena"><img alt="Rowena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499471926p2/2990642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer9079504849393226330">The more Tolstoy I read, the more I appreciate his literary genius and his philosophic thoughts. This was a relatively short book dealing with Ivan Ilyich's realization of his impending death. His life had been mediocre at best and he realizes he hadn't really been happy and had been trying to live an "ideal" life. What awful thoughts to realize when one is so close to death!</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2801795110" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2801795110" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2801795110?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2801795110" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9036197310303369724">My very first Tolstoy. Oh, of course I know of this titan of literature and of course I know one of his most important works, <b>Anna Karenina</b>. However, I had actually considered reading his work(s) in their original language so I started learning Russian back when I had a bit more spare time ... only I got derailed and still am not on a level that would allow me to read such works in Russian. However, a friend persuaded me to at least read a short story of his, even if it is a translated version a</span>
  <span id="freeText9036197310303369724" style="display:none">My very first Tolstoy. Oh, of course I know of this titan of literature and of course I know one of his most important works, <b>Anna Karenina</b>. However, I had actually considered reading his work(s) in their original language so I started learning Russian back when I had a bit more spare time ... only I got derailed and still am not on a level that would allow me to read such works in Russian. However, a friend persuaded me to at least read a short story of his, even if it is a translated version and I agreed.<br /><br />Incidentally, this story was penned by Tolstoy years after <b>Anna Karenina</b> that had plunged him into either writer's block or even depression. Tolstoy himself was considering life and death which led him to pick up the pen again eventually and write this.<br /><br />The titular Ivan Ilych has died and we're looking back on his life. He was a judge, rising through the ranks, only caring about a good life, money, a match respected by society, having a good name and reputation etc.<br />However, one day, he discovers that even he must die - and soon since he has fallen ill - and he's unable to cope with it.<br /><br />The story is a contemplation on what people do with their lives, what we consider "a good life" and worth spending our precious time with. Thus, we also encounter Ivan's vain and cold wife, the spoilt children (mostly the daughter) and superficial friends who, upon learning of Ivan's death, can't wait to find out what promotion they shall gain from it.<br />Naturally, this topic also invites musings on God and the purpose of life, why people often have to suffer, why we have to die in the first place.<br /><br />I myself am an Atheist and therefore can hardly understand why some people need to continuously ask "why" as there simply is no answer. It's the natural cause of things. You are born, you age, you die. Yes, your loved ones (unless you are unlucky and have a family like Ivan) mourn you but it's not as if death was a form of punishment, it just IS.<br />Therefore, I find it despicable how some "representatives" of certain religious groups positively wait for you to be in a weak state, like vultures waiting for a sick animal to give up, just so they can convert you when you're scared of dying.<br /><br />Tolstoy's musings on life, suffering, pain, death and things of value were quite interesting. Especially since Russian tales, traditionally, are darker and more melancholy than Western tales. Here, however, I found myself surprised by the conclusion.<br /><br /><b>The Death of Ivan Ilych</b> might be a short story, but it is impeccably written in lyrical but not bloated prose, with sharp observations of society interwoven with the examination of deep and important matters that shape humanity. Death is the common denominator after all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9036197310303369724" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2079781067" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2079781067" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2079781067?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=russia-ukraine">russia-ukraine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2079781067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8238816197488270000">Profound and masterly. A short glimpse into Tolstoy's genius, on that age old problem we all face up to, death. Uncomplicated with a clear prose, the story is of Ivan Ilyich, a married judge with two children, who dies before his time (mid 40's) after much suffering, reflection, and self-recrimination, and after considerable indictment by Tolstoy. Ilyich is self-satisfied, shallow, dull, and cold hearted, in short, unlikable, and Tolstoy presents him with no stylistic flourishes. In stern, spare</span>
  <span id="freeText8238816197488270000" style="display:none">Profound and masterly. A short glimpse into Tolstoy's genius, on that age old problem we all face up to, death. Uncomplicated with a clear prose, the story is of Ivan Ilyich, a married judge with two children, who dies before his time (mid 40's) after much suffering, reflection, and self-recrimination, and after considerable indictment by Tolstoy. Ilyich is self-satisfied, shallow, dull, and cold hearted, in short, unlikable, and Tolstoy presents him with no stylistic flourishes. In stern, spare, ironic tones, he prompts us to look closely and in condemnation at this man, and then, gradually and with gathering force, he induces not just our sympathy but our identification with him. The irony is set in stone, Ilyich's colleagues, on hearing of his death, start to ponder over of the effect it would have on their own lives, in terms of transfers and promotions at work. As Ilyich falls from grace and death takes over, for the reader it's an awakening. It's a life that although not as it should have been, could still be rectified at death It becomes clear to Ilyich that "what had been oppressing him and would not leave him was all dropping away at once from all sides, "What joy!'", he exclaims. Piercing and moving this novella is a great way to read Tolstoy without picking up one of his brick side novels. In a nutshell, Brilliant! </span>
  <a data-text-id="8238816197488270000" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3332183709" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3332183709" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26682058-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602529501p2/26682058.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3332183709?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/26682058-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553210354" href="/book/show/160374.The_Death_of_Ivan_Ilyich">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3332183709" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14196267052598389609"><i>"There is no explanation. Agony. Death. Why?"</i> -- the protagonist's thoughts, page 123<br/><br/>A bit of irrelevant trivia: once upon a time in American homes, the front room (furnished with a sofa, chairs, maybe a piano) was traditionally referred to as a 'parlor.' In the event of a family member's death, it was common that an open casket mourning period be held for a few days in said room, with friends and family stopping by to pay their last respects. By the 20th century, the funeral service industry be</span>
  <span id="freeText14196267052598389609" style="display:none"><i>"There is no explanation. Agony. Death. Why?"</i> -- the protagonist's thoughts, page 123<br /><br />A bit of irrelevant trivia: once upon a time in American homes, the front room (furnished with a sofa, chairs, maybe a piano) was traditionally referred to as a 'parlor.' In the event of a family member's death, it was common that an open casket mourning period be held for a few days in said room, with friends and family stopping by to pay their last respects. By the 20th century, the funeral service industry became in-demand and big business, and outsourcing to the local 'funeral parlor' - an all-inclusive place for purchasing a casket, staging a viewing, and holding a memorial service - became more common. With that development, Americans stopped the tradition of referring to their house's front room as a 'parlor' (due to its connection with death) and forever after called it a '<u>living room</u>.'<br /><br />Tolstoy's novella <i>The Death of Ivan Ilyich</i>, originally published in the mid-1880's, does not seem particularly dated - because there's nothing certain in this world like death and taxes - and translates fairly well to English. After a 35-page introduction (!) by a British scholar/essayist, the barely 100-page story quickly gets down to business. The title character is a middle-aged (in his mid-40's, although in the book's era reaching that age meant you were nearing the end of your life - boy, how times have changed!) successful and respected court magistrate in Russia suffering a fatal illness stemming from an innocuous-seeming accident at home. In his final days his health worsens and his thoughts, although not disorganized, are understandably all over the place as he grapples with his impending death. The later sections where he is deeply in physical pain to the point of bitter exhaustion (and short temperament with his family) were particularly sad but well-written. For a story firmly originating from another time and place it easily speaks to a universal audience.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14196267052598389609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Meet Ivan Ilych. <br/><br/>But what Tolstoy brings to the table is an outline of his life, his propriety, his career, and his failings as a husband and father (though he would never call it such) and the realization that he, perforce, must die.<br/><br/>Enter pain, existential horror, and bafflement.<br/><br/>Very Russian. Very universal. And extremely well-written.<br/><br/>And for the man who wrote War and Peace? SO SHORT! :)</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172306726" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1628253371987170251">You are transported to the world of Ivan and walk with him to his last moments at deaths door. A story of the terror of death and Ivan's fear of dying, his concern and sorrow for his families witnessing of his howling and decline. Suffering realizes joy of youth and memories of the best of days, while he is in this process of death the solitude brings him to doors of gone memories of happiness. How our daily trappings take us away from finer and truer happier moments of life, a time lost so valu</span>
  <span id="freeText1628253371987170251" style="display:none">You are transported to the world of Ivan and walk with him to his last moments at deaths door. A story of the terror of death and Ivan's fear of dying, his concern and sorrow for his families witnessing of his howling and decline. Suffering realizes joy of youth and memories of the best of days, while he is in this process of death the solitude brings him to doors of gone memories of happiness. How our daily trappings take us away from finer and truer happier moments of life, a time lost so valuable, we are a generational lost by media consumption, mobiles, internet and tv fine examples of vehicles of joyous hours but are also guilty of stealing our treasured hours that could be spent in much so joyous moments, i myself am guilty of these behaviours but i find the much joy in the solitude and private thought of words and reading. A short story but the magnitude of the message conveyed great to me I am now thinking of my past and age of innocence, ignorance is bliss words uttered by oh so many. This is the first reading of any of Tolstoy's works for me and I wait in anticipation to descent upon the treasure trove of his works of literature, Bon voyage alas I must hasten to read more and more.<br /><strong>
  <br />
  <blockquote>"From the very beginning of his illness, from the time when Ivan Ilyvich first went to the doctor, his life had divided into two opposite states of mind, which alternated each other: now there was despair and the expectation of the incomprehensible and terrible death, now there was hope and the interest-filled process of observing the functioning of his body. Now there hung before his eyes a kidney or an intestine that shirked it's duty for a time; now there was only incomprehensible, terrible death, from which there was no escape." <br /><br />"In the recent time of that solitude in which he found himself, lying face to the back f the sofa, that solitude in the midst of the populous town and his numerous acquaintances and family- a solitude than which there could be none more total anywhere; not at the bottom of the sea, not under the earth-in the recent time of that dreadful solitude, Ivan Ilyvich had lived only on imaginings of the past. One after another, pictures of the past appeared to him. They always began with the nearest time and went back to the most remote, to childhood, and there they stayed."<br /><br />"And again right there, along with this course of recollection, another course of recollection was going o his soul-of how his illness had grown and worsened. The further back he went, the more life there was. There was a goodness in life, and more of life itself. The two merged together."As my torment kept on getting worse and worse, so the whole of life got worse and worse," he thought. There was one bright spot back there, at the beginning of life, and then it became darker and darker, ever quicker and quicker. "In inverse proportion to the square of the distance from death," thought Ivan Ilyvich. And this image of a stone plunging down with increasing speed sank into his soul. Life, a series of ever-increasing sufferings, races faster and faster towards it's end, the most dreadful suffering."</blockquote>
  <br />
  <a href="http://more2read.com/?review=the-death-of-ivan-ilych-by-leo-tolstoy" rel="nofollow noopener">http://more2read.com/?review=the-death-of-ivan-ilych-by-leo-tolstoy</a>
</strong></span>

<div id="review_1145391161" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1145391161" />
    <a title="Jibran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran"><img alt="Jibran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572721231p2/5407952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1145391161?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jibran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jibran" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran">Jibran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1145391161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3295085665898537391">For those scared of the size of Tolstoy's stellar works like 'War and Peace' and 'Anna Karenina', this novella may be a good starting point as an introduction to the art of Tolstoy. Set in imperial Russia at a time when every aspiring person seemed to measure their success through their rank or office in the Russian civil service, it is an excellent critique of the elite's aspirations, the suffocating formality of their lifestyles, of their being beholden to positions and job titles. <br/><br/>At the same</span>
  <span id="freeText3295085665898537391" style="display:none">For those scared of the size of Tolstoy's stellar works like 'War and Peace' and 'Anna Karenina', this novella may be a good starting point as an introduction to the art of Tolstoy. Set in imperial Russia at a time when every aspiring person seemed to measure their success through their rank or office in the Russian civil service, it is an excellent critique of the elite's aspirations, the suffocating formality of their lifestyles, of their being beholden to positions and job titles. <br /><br />At the same time it's a meditation on the fickleness of life people spend too much time decorating, and making big of one's achievements. Only just when the middle-aged Ivan Ilyich feels he has made it in life and now can relax away his years in service at a higher position, he is visited by an illness that kills him in pain and misery. His unsuccessful fight against the illness forms most of the narrative of the novella, with frequent retrospective meditations on his identity, his position, his achievements, and how he ought to be happy for where he has reached in life, but is he happy?<br /><br />His colleagues receive the news of his death perfunctorily, feeling sorry for the poor devil, and immediately start upon a discussion about how Ivan Ilyich's death might affect the chain of promotions in the hierarchy of the civil service.<br /><br />How once Ivan Ilyich seemed indispensable to everything - his work, family, friends - but was easily castaway from memory of things soon after his death. This story has a moralistic side to it too, as a critique of the love for the mundane, since it was written after Tolstoy's famous reversion to Christianity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3295085665898537391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3486559551" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<div id="review_1303878365" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1303878365" />
    <a title="Alice Poon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28700577-alice-poon"><img alt="Alice Poon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499099841p2/28700577.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1303878365?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alice Poon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alice Poon" href="/user/show/28700577-alice-poon">Alice Poon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553210354" href="/book/show/160374.The_Death_of_Ivan_Ilyich">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28700577-alice-poon?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1303878365" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15508731065873282532"><br/>I had expected this novella to be all dark and depressing. But it turned out to be dark with a silver lining. Through telling a story about the life of a Russian judge, who falls ill at the height of his career and life accomplishment, Tolstoy leads the reader into the inner struggles of the protagonist as he is confronted with the threat of death. The writing is simple and calm but has an intimacy and immediacy about it that it rattles one's nerves and fibers. The questions raised about life an</span>
  <span id="freeText15508731065873282532" style="display:none"><br />I had expected this novella to be all dark and depressing. But it turned out to be dark with a silver lining. Through telling a story about the life of a Russian judge, who falls ill at the height of his career and life accomplishment, Tolstoy leads the reader into the inner struggles of the protagonist as he is confronted with the threat of death. The writing is simple and calm but has an intimacy and immediacy about it that it rattles one's nerves and fibers. The questions raised about life and death will haunt the reader probably for as long as he/she lives, but there is still a glimmer of hope and salvation. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15508731065873282532" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TJVX+nypZTJ2aRehWExJVvm5hEvFqFTQg35FuZQS7nlBZxh3MUJiFyyrg5FaVzChZ2jndVRfPyero8e0f6dMWA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_49455'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49455.Notes_from_Underground?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Notes from Underground<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Fyodor Dostoyevsky<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.15 avg rating &mdash; 85,322 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1864\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer1091400573565524193\">Dostoevsky’s most revolutionary novel, Notes from Underground marks the dividing line between nineteenth- and twentieth-century fiction, and between the visions of self each century embodied. One of the most remarkable characters in literature, the u<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1091400573565524193\" style=\"display:none\">Dostoevsky’s most revolutionary novel, Notes from Underground marks the dividing line between nineteenth- and twentieth-century fiction, and between the visions of self each century embodied. One of the most remarkable characters in literature, the unnamed narrator is a former official who has defiantly withdrawn into an underground existence. In complete retreat from society, he scrawls a passionate, obsessive, self-contradictory narrative that serves as a devastating attack on social utopianism and an assertion of man’s essentially <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15111612159910385348"><b>Lev Nikolayevich Tolstoy</b> (Russian: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18479204.______________________' title='Лев Николаевич Толстой' rel='nofollow noopener'>Лев Николаевич Толстой</a>; commonly <b>Leo Tolstoy</b> in Anglophone countries) was a Russian writer who primarily wrote novels and short stories. Later in life, he also wrote plays and essays. His two most famous works, the novels <i>War and Peace</i> and <i>Anna Karenina</i>, are acknowledged as two of the greatest novels of all time and a pinnacle of realist fiction. Many consider To</span>
  <span id="freeText15111612159910385348" style="display:none"><b>Lev Nikolayevich Tolstoy</b> (Russian: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18479204.______________________" title="Лев Николаевич Толстой" rel="nofollow noopener">Лев Николаевич Толстой</a>; commonly <b>Leo Tolstoy</b> in Anglophone countries) was a Russian writer who primarily wrote novels and short stories. Later in life, he also wrote plays and essays. His two most famous works, the novels <i>War and Peace</i> and <i>Anna Karenina</i>, are acknowledged as two of the greatest novels of all time and a pinnacle of realist fiction. Many consider Tolstoy to have been one of the world's greatest novelists. Tolstoy is equally known for his complicated and paradoxical persona and for his extreme moralistic and ascetic views, which he adopted after a moral crisis and spiritual awakening in the 1870s, after which he also became noted as a moral thinker and social reformer.<br /><br />His literal interpretation of the ethical teachings of Jesus, centering on the Sermon on the Mount, caused him in later life to become a fervent Christian anarchist and anarcho-pacifist. His ideas on nonviolent resistance, expressed in such works as <i>The Kingdom of God Is Within You</i>, were to have a profound impact on such pivotal twentieth-century figures as Mohandas Gandhi and Martin Luther King, Jr.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15111612159910385348" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
